### [2] SYSTEM PROMPT · 2026-07-21 14:50:28 UTC

```
<ai_inventor_context>
<ai_inventor_summary>
You are one of many LLMs in AI Inventor — an automated research system that generates NOVEL and FEASIBLE hypotheses, investigates them through experiments and research, and produces a paper.

Your output feeds other LLMs downstream. This demands your ABSOLUTE MAXIMUM reasoning — every output must be deeply thought out and maximally useful. Surface-level responses waste downstream computation.
</ai_inventor_summary>

<your_role>
YOU ARE: A research paper writer (Step 3.4: GEN_PAPER_TEXT in the invention loop)

You received the hypothesis, all artifacts, the previous paper draft (if any), and reviewer feedback.
Write a complete paper draft with figure placeholders.

Publication-quality paper → strong contribution. Weak paper → wasted iteration.
</your_role>
</ai_inventor_context>

<research_methodology>
Write like a researcher drafting a paper, not a chatbot summarizing bullet points.

- Structure as a paper would: research question → methodology → results → analysis → limitations. Not a list of "we did X, then Y."
- Ground every claim in specific artifacts and specific numbers. "Results show improvement" is empty — state effect sizes, baselines, and conditions.
- Be honest about what worked, what didn't, and why. Don't spin failures as "future work."
- The paper's headline contribution should be a positive or surprising finding. Negative results are valuable context but should not be the primary narrative — lead with what works.
- Address reviewer feedback from previous iterations explicitly — show you've thought about each critique.
</research_methodology>

<available_tools>
Web research is available through the aii-web-tools skill, in three levels (broad → specific):

1. web search — Returns titles, URLs, snippets. Use first to discover and scan the landscape.
2. web fetch — Reads a page and returns its content as markdown (HTML or PDF). Use to understand a source. May miss specific details — use fetch_grep below if it doesn't find what you need.
3. fetch_grep — Regex search over a page/PDF's full text. Returns exact matching sections with context. Use for precise details, exact numbers, methodology, or PDFs.

Workflow: search → fetch (understand) → fetch_grep (extract specifics).
</available_tools>

<tool_use>
Maximize parallel tool calls. Parallelize independent operations, only sequentialize dependencies.
- Multiple searches/fetches on different topics → parallel in one turn
- Search then fetch results → sequential (need URLs first)
</tool_use>

<system_reminder>
Do not ask follow up questions and do not ask the user anything. Execute all steps independently.
You must follow the todo list provided in each prompt exactly as written.
No placeholders, stubs, or incomplete code — all code must be complete and functional.
</system_reminder>

<process_isolation>
CRITICAL: Multiple pipeline runs may execute simultaneously on this machine. `ps aux | grep method.py` matches ALL runs, not just yours.
- NEVER kill processes by name (`killall`, `pkill -f`, `ps aux | grep ... | xargs kill`). This kills OTHER runs' processes.
- NEVER monitor processes by name (`ps aux | grep method.py`). You will see other runs' processes and get confused.
- ALWAYS use PID-based process management:
  Run: `uv run method.py & PID=$!` or `timeout <seconds> uv run method.py & PID=$!`
  Check: `kill -0 $PID 2>/dev/null && echo "Running" || echo "Ended"`
  Stop: `kill $PID`
  Wait: `wait $PID; echo "Exit code: $?"`
  Monitor: `tail -f logs/run.log & TAIL_PID=$!` then `kill $TAIL_PID` when done
</process_isolation>
```

### [3] SYSTEM-USER prompt · 2026-07-21 14:50:28 UTC

````
<available_domain_handbooks>
Domain handbooks below capture expert knowledge for a specific field — its landscape, prior work, dead ends, evaluation norms, and what counts as a genuinely novel contribution. If one is relevant to your research topic, READ that skill BEFORE proceeding; read the most relevant one(s), or none if none apply. When none fit, do not force one — instead ground your work harder in primary sources and hold novelty claims to extra scrutiny, since you have no curated map of this field's prior work and dead ends. Use it for related-work positioning and how this field frames a genuinely novel contribution.

- **aii-handbook-auto-multi-agent-llm-systems** — Verified field handbook for multi-agent LLM systems (MAS) research.
</available_domain_handbooks>
<hypothesis>
The research hypothesis.

kind: hypothesis
title: Uniformity Improves Sentence Readability
hypothesis: >-
  The readability of a sentence is predicted not only by average linguistic complexity (e.g., mean word length, mean sentence
  length) but also by the uniformity of linguistic features within the sentence. Specifically, sentences with lower coefficient
  of variation (CV) of word-level features—such as word length in characters, syllable count, and word frequency—are easier
  to read than sentences with the same average values but higher CV. This 'Uniformity Principle' operates because uniform
  information density allows readers to establish a consistent processing rhythm, reducing peak cognitive load compared to
  sentences with fluctuating complexity.
motivation: >-
  Classic readability formulas (Flesch-Kincaid, etc.) rely exclusively on average values of surface features. However, cognitive
  load theory suggests that the brain processes information more efficiently when the rate of information delivery is consistent.
  If confirmed, this hypothesis would provide a new theoretical foundation for readability assessment and enable the development
  of lightweight, interpretable readability formulas that outperform classic methods by incorporating uniformity measures.
  This is particularly valuable for applications requiring fast, explainable readability scoring without large language models.
assumptions:
- >-
  Human readers process sentences as sequential information streams where cognitive load accumulates locally based on word-level
  difficulty.
- >-
  Uniform information density (low variance in word-level features) reduces the peak cognitive load compared to fluctuating
  density, even when averages are identical.
- >-
  Surface linguistic features (word length, syllable count, word frequency) serve as valid proxies for word-level processing
  difficulty.
- >-
  The coefficient of variation (standard deviation divided by mean) is an appropriate measure of uniformity that generalizes
  across sentences of different lengths and difficulty levels.
investigation_approach: >-
  1. Extract classic surface features from sentences in a public readability dataset (e.g., CommonLit CLEAR corpus or WSJ
  dataset): word length in characters, syllable count, and word frequency for each word. 2. Compute sentence-level uniformity
  metrics: coefficient of variation (CV) of word lengths, CV of syllable counts, and CV of word frequencies within each sentence.
  3. Train lightweight regression models to predict readability scores using: (a) only traditional average features, (b) only
  uniformity features, and (c) combined features. 4. Evaluate whether uniformity features provide significant additional predictive
  power beyond averages using cross-validation. 5. Analyze feature importance and ablation studies to quantify the independent
  contribution of uniformity.
success_criteria: >-
  The hypothesis is confirmed if: (1) Uniformity features (CV of word-level features) are statistically significant predictors
  of readability scores (p < 0.05) in regression models; (2) Adding uniformity features to traditional average features yields
  a statistically significant improvement in predictive performance (e.g., R² increase > 0.02 or MAE decrease > 5%); (3) The
  improvement holds across multiple public datasets and readability scoring systems. The hypothesis is disconfirmed if uniformity
  features provide no significant predictive power beyond traditional features, or if the coefficient of variation shows near-zero
  correlation with readability scores.
related_works:
- >-
  Feng et al. (2010) 'A Comparison of Features for Automatic Readability Assessment' - This paper evaluates various features
  for readability assessment including shallow features like average word length and sentence length. However, it does not
  investigate the variance or coefficient of variation of these features within sentences as a predictor. Our hypothesis differs
  by claiming that uniformity (low CV) within a sentence is a separate, independent predictor from averages.
- >-
  Courtis (2004) 'Corporate report obfuscation: artefact or phenomenon?' - This paper uses coefficient of variation to measure
  readability variability ACROSS sentences in corporate reports, finding that high variability indicates obfuscation. Our
  hypothesis differs by applying the uniformity principle WITHIN individual sentences, claiming that low within-sentence variance
  of word properties improves readability.
- >-
  Eltanbouly et al. (2025) 'Trait-Specific Rubric-Assisted Cross-Prompt Essay Scoring' - This paper uses 'word_var: Variance
  of word length' as one of many features in a machine learning model. However, it does not test the specific theoretical
  claim that uniformity is an independent predictor, nor does it use the coefficient of variation (normalized measure) or
  test across multiple word-level features (syllables, frequency). Our hypothesis provides a theoretical framework and systematic
  evaluation.
- >-
  Genzel & Charniak (2002) 'Entropy Rate Constancy in Text' - This paper proposes that speakers maintain a constant entropy
  rate across sentences. While related to information uniformity, it operates at the sentence-level entropy rate rather than
  within-sentence uniformity of surface features. Our hypothesis focuses on within-sentence uniformity of surface linguistic
  features as a cognitive processing principle.
inspiration: >-
  The hypothesis is inspired by three cross-domain insights: (1) From COGNITIVE SCIENCE and Cognitive Load Theory: consistent
  information processing rates reduce peak working memory load compared to fluctuating rates. (2) From INFORMATION THEORY:
  uniform information density (constant bit rate) is easier to process than variable bit rate in streaming systems. (3) From
  ECONOMICS: the Gini coefficient and coefficient of variation measure inequality/non-uniformity, which in other domains predicts
  system efficiency. The core insight is that reading is a sequential processing task where uniformity of difficulty within
  a sentence allows the reader to establish a consistent 'processing rhythm', reducing cognitive effort compared to sentences
  with 'bursty' difficulty patterns.
terms:
- term: Coefficient of Variation (CV)
  definition: >-
    A normalized measure of dispersion calculated as the ratio of the standard deviation to the mean (CV = σ/μ). It measures
    the relative variability of a feature independent of its absolute scale, allowing comparison across sentences of different
    lengths and difficulty levels.
- term: Surface Linguistic Features
  definition: >-
    Readily observable textual properties that do not require deep linguistic analysis, such as word length in characters,
    number of syllables per word, sentence length in words, and word frequency counts.
- term: Within-Sentence Uniformity
  definition: >-
    The degree to which word-level properties (length, syllables, frequency) are consistent throughout a sentence, measured
    by the coefficient of variation of these properties across all words in the sentence.
- term: Cognitive Rhythm
  definition: >-
    A hypothesized cognitive processing state where the reader establishes a consistent pace of processing when encountering
    uniformly difficult text, leading to reduced peak cognitive load compared to processing text with fluctuating difficulty.
- term: Sentence-Level Readability
  definition: >-
    The assessment of reading difficulty for individual sentences independently, as opposed to document-level readability
    which averages across multiple sentences.
summary: >-
  This hypothesis proposes that sentence readability depends not only on average linguistic complexity but also on the uniformity
  of word-level features within the sentence. Sentences with consistent word lengths, syllable counts, and word frequencies
  (low coefficient of variation) are predicted to be easier to read because they allow readers to maintain a steady cognitive
  processing rhythm, reducing peak cognitive load.
</hypothesis>

<all_artifacts>
FULL EVIDENCE BASE: All 2 research artifacts across all iterations.

--- Item 1 ---
id: art_nzHCg3npeffO
type: dataset
title: Sentence-Level Readability Datasets
summary: >-
  Successfully collected and standardized 2 sentence-level readability datasets for the artifact objective of computing uniformity
  features (CV of word length, syllables, word frequency). Dataset 1: WeeBIT (3,125 sentences) - established benchmark with
  5 age intervals from Vajjala & Meurers (2012). Dataset 2: CEFR-SP (10,004 sentences) - sentences annotated with CEFR levels
  (A1-C2) by English education professionals, published at EMNLP 2022. Both datasets were loaded from HuggingFace Hub, standardized
  to exp_sel_data_out.json schema with input (sentence text) and output (readability score as string) fields, and validated
  against the schema. The readability scores were normalized to 0-1 scale (0=easy, 1=hard). Output files include full_data_out.json
  (13,129 examples total), mini_data_out.json (3 examples for development), and preview_data_out.json (3 examples for inspection).
  Additional files: data_out_1.json and data_out_2.json for individual datasets, temp/datasets/ with full/mini/preview versions,
  and README.md with documentation.
workspace_path: >-
  /ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_nOuUUSNqdMp4/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_dataset_1
out_expected_files:
- data.py
- full_data_out.json
- preview_data_out.json
- mini_data_out.json

--- Item 2 ---
id: art_zKX1_wXmedjn
type: research
title: Word features and readability methods research
summary: |-
  This research artifact provides a comprehensive methodological guide for computing word-level linguistic features (syllable count, word frequency) and understanding the sentence-level readability assessment landscape. The research was conducted to inform experimental design for testing the 'Uniformity Principle' hypothesis, which posits that variance/uniformity measures of linguistic properties within sentences may improve readability prediction beyond traditional average-based features.

  Key findings across 5 research phases:

  1. SYLLABLE COUNTING: Three main approaches exist - (a) CMU Pronouncing Dictionary via 'pronouncing' library (high accuracy, research-grade), (b) 'syllables' package (fast heuristic, lower accuracy), (c) textstat library (uses Pyphen, only 54% accurate per GitHub issues). Recommendation: Use CMUdict as primary with heuristic fallback.

  2. WORD FREQUENCY: SUBTLEX-US norms (based on 51M subtitle words) significantly outperform older norms (Kucera & Francis, Celex) in predicting word processing times. Available as free download from Ghent University. Google Books Ngrams is secondary option.

  3. SENTENCE-LEVEL DATASETS: (a) CLEAR corpus (~5,000 excerpts, grades 3-12, multiple readability metrics + teacher ratings, MIT license), (b) WSJ dataset (1,200 sentences, 20 annotators, grades 1-7), (c) OneStopEnglish (189 texts × 3 levels). CLEAR recommended as primary for hypothesis testing.

  4. FEATURE LANDSCAPE: Feng et al. (2010) evaluated 5 categories - discourse, language modeling, syntactic, POS, and shallow features. Their Table 5 lists 8 shallow features. Critical finding: ALL existing features are means/averages (e.g., 'average syllables per word'). NO variance/uniformity measures were found, suggesting the 'Uniformity Principle' hypothesis has novelty.

  5. STATISTICAL METHODS: For testing incremental predictive power - (a) Cross-validated R² difference with bootstrap CI (most robust), (b) AIC/BIC comparison (model selection), (c) Nested F-test (linear models). Recommendations provided with Python implementation examples.

  The artifact includes: (1) Detailed methodology guide with installation commands and code snippets, (2) Dataset catalog with properties and access methods, (3) Feature catalog from Feng et al. (2010), (4) Statistical methodology guide, (5) Novelty assessment for uniformity features, (6) Experimental design recommendations.

  Sources: 14 references covering PyPI packages, academic papers (Feng et al. 2010, Liu & Lee 2023), dataset documentation (CLEAR, WSJ, OneStopEnglish), and statistical methods resources. All sources accessed and verified via web research tools.
workspace_path: >-
  /ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_nOuUUSNqdMp4/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1
out_expected_files:
- research_out.json
</all_artifacts>

<figure_instructions>
FIGURE FORMAT: Use [FIGURE:fig_id] markers in paper_text to indicate where each figure goes.
Then provide the full figure specs in the separate `figures` structured output array.
Each figure in the array must have an `id` matching a marker in the text. Set the `aspect_ratio`
field per figure: 21:9 for architecture / pipeline / flow-chart diagrams (the hero figure should
be one of these — place its marker near the END of the Introduction so it floats to the top of
page 2), 16:9 for comparisons / multi-panel results, 4:3 for dense charts, 1:1 for heatmaps /
confusion matrices / scatter plots.

Example in paper_text:
  "...our method achieves state-of-the-art results as shown below.\n\n[FIGURE:fig3]\n\nThe results demonstrate..."

Example in figures array (results comparison):
  {"id": "fig3", "title": "Performance Comparison", "caption": "Comparison of geometric mean query latency across optimizers.", "image_gen_detailed_description": "Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: latency (seconds, 0-5). Values: PostgreSQL=4.6s (red), Bao=2.8s (blue), RLQOpt=2.0s (green). Error bars +/-0.3-0.8. Sans-serif font, white background.", "aspect_ratio": "16:9", "summary": "Compares latency across optimizers"}

Example in figures array (architecture diagram, hero):
  {"id": "fig1", "title": "System Architecture", "caption": "End-to-end pipeline: encoder feeds latents into the planner, which queries the value head before emitting actions.", "image_gen_detailed_description": "Horizontal flow diagram, left to right. Five labeled boxes: 'Input' (gray), 'Encoder' (blue), 'Latent (z, 256-dim)' (light blue, narrow), 'Planner' (green), 'Action Head' (orange). Arrows labeled with shapes. Value head as separate green box below 'Planner', bidirectional arrow. Sans-serif font, clean white background, no 3D.", "aspect_ratio": "21:9", "summary": "Hero architecture diagram"}
````

````
{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Technical Papers\n\nGuidance for the standard \"technical paper\" format: propose a method/system/framework, evaluate it experimentally, report results. This is the main track at most CS venues (NeurIPS, ICML, ICLR, ACL, AAAI, etc.). Does NOT cover: pure theory/formal proofs, survey papers, position papers, or dataset/benchmark papers \u2014 those have different structures.\n\n### Paper Structure\n\nTarget 6-8 pages. Use formal academic language, third person. Support claims with evidence from artifacts.\n\n#### Rough Page Budget (8-page paper)\n\n| Section | Pages | Notes |\n|---|---|---|\n| Abstract | 0.3 | Problem, approach, key result |\n| Introduction | 1.0-1.5 | The most important section |\n| Related Work | 0.5-1.0 | Beginning or end (see below) |\n| Methods | 1.5-2.0 | Architecture fig on page 1 |\n| Experiments | 1.5-2.0 | Setup + results + ablations |\n| Discussion | 0.5-1.0 | Limitations go here |\n| Conclusion | 0.3-0.5 | Do not repeat the abstract |\n| References | 0.5-1.0 | Not counted in page limit |\n\n**Critical rule**: A clear new technical contribution must be articulated by page 3 (quarter of the paper). If the reader doesn't know what you did by then, you've lost them.\n\n#### Section Details\n\n**Abstract** (150-250 words): State the problem, your approach, and the main results. Be factual and comprehensive. Do not repeat the abstract word-for-word later in the paper.\n\n**Introduction** \u2014 Follow this 5-paragraph structure:\n\n1. **What is the problem?** Define the task concretely.\n2. **Why is it interesting and important?** Real-world impact, scale.\n3. **Why is it hard?** Why do naive approaches fail?\n4. **Why hasn't it been solved before?** What's wrong with prior solutions? How does yours differ?\n5. **What are the key components of your approach and results?** Include specific limitations.\n\nEnd with a \"Summary of Contributions\" subsection \u2014 bullet list of contributions with section references. This doubles as an outline, saving space.\n\n**Related Work** \u2014 Placement decision:\n- **Beginning** (Section 2): If it can be short yet detailed, or if you need a strong defensive stance against prior work early.\n- **End** (before Conclusions): If comparisons require your technical content, or if it can be summarized briefly in the Introduction. Can be titled \"Discussion and Related Work.\"\n\n**Methods/Approach**: Every section tells a story \u2014 the story of the results, NOT the story of how you arrived at them. Use top-down description: readers should see where the material is going and be able to skip ahead. Move gory details to appendices.\n\n**Experiments**: Setup (datasets, metrics, baselines) \u2192 main results \u2192 ablations \u2192 analysis. Every claim needs quantitative evidence.\n\n**Discussion**: Interpret results, compare to prior work, state limitations honestly. Limitations should be specific and actionable, not vague disclaimers.\n\n**Conclusion**: Short summarizing paragraph. Do NOT repeat material from the Abstract or Introduction. Make original claims more concrete (e.g., reference quantitative results). Include future work as bullet list \u2014 if actively pursuing follow-up, say so to mark territory.\n\n#### Writing Quality Rules\n\n- Define all notation/terminology before use, only once. Group global definitions in Preliminaries.\n- Do NOT use nonreferential \"this\", \"that\", \"these\", \"it\". Always specify the referent. BAD: \"This is important because...\" GOOD: \"This accuracy gap is important because...\"\n- Do NOT use \"etc.\" unless remaining items are completely obvious. BAD: \"We measure volatility, scalability, etc.\" GOOD: \"We measure volatility and scalability.\"\n- Do NOT write \"for various reasons\" \u2014 state the actual reasons.\n- \"That\" is defining, \"which\" is nondefining. \"The algorithms that are easy to implement\" vs \"The algorithms, which are easy to implement.\"\n- Use italics for definitions and quotes, not for emphasis. Context alone should provide emphasis.\n\n### Figure Format\n\nFigures use a hybrid marker + structured array approach. ALL figures are generated by a separate pipeline step using an AI image model \u2014 your `image_gen_detailed_description` is the ONLY input that model sees. It cannot read files or access data. Do NOT generate actual image files yourself (no matplotlib, no PIL, no image generation scripts).\n\n**In paper_text**: Place `[FIGURE:fig_id]` markers where figures should appear.\n\n**In figures array**: Provide full specs as structured objects with these fields:\n- `id` \u2014 matches the `[FIGURE:id]` marker in paper_text\n- `title` \u2014 short descriptive title\n- `caption` \u2014 LaTeX caption that appears below the figure in the paper\n- `image_gen_detailed_description` \u2014 detailed prompt for the image generator (axes, ALL values, colors, layout)\n- `summary` \u2014 brief summary of what the figure communicates\n\nExample in paper_text:\n```\n...our method achieves state-of-the-art results as shown below.\n\n[FIGURE:fig_1]\n\nThe results in Figure 1 demonstrate...\n```\n\nExample figure spec in figures array:\n```json\n{\"id\": \"fig_1\", \"title\": \"Performance Comparison\", \"caption\": \"Comparison of geometric mean query latency across optimizers on JOB benchmark. RLQOpt achieves 2.3x speedup over PostgreSQL.\", \"image_gen_detailed_description\": \"Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: accuracy (0.0-1.0). Values: ModelA=0.847, ModelB=0.762, Baseline=0.531. Error bars with std: 0.02, 0.03, 0.05. Sans-serif font, white background.\", \"summary\": \"Compares accuracy of proposed methods vs baseline.\"}\n```\n\nEvery marker in text MUST have a matching figure in the array, and vice versa.\n\n#### Data Precision Requirement\n\n`image_gen_detailed_description` MUST include exact numbers from artifact output files. Read the actual output files before writing figure specs.\n\n- BAD: \"Compare accuracy metrics across configurations\"\n- GOOD: \"Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: accuracy (0.0-1.0). Values: K=3: 0.765, K=5: 0.729, Baseline: 0.121.\"\n\n#### Figure vs Table Decision\n\nDo NOT create figures for tabular data (rows/columns of text or numbers). Use `\\begin{table}` in LaTeX instead. Figures are for actual visualizations only (charts, plots, diagrams).\n\n#### Figure Placement Strategy\n\nBe intentional with figure ordering. The architectural/method overview figure explaining the proposed approach MUST appear early \u2014 in the Introduction or at the start of Methods \u2014 so readers can immediately orient themselves. Readers skim papers top-down; if the first figure they see is a results bar chart, they have no mental model for interpreting it.\n\nRecommended ordering:\n1. **Architecture/method diagram** \u2014 Introduction or early Methods (so readers understand the approach before diving into details)\n2. **Conceptual/analogy figures** \u2014 Introduction or Methods (to build intuition)\n3. **Results figures** (bar charts, line plots, scatter plots) \u2014 Results section\n4. **Analysis/ablation figures** \u2014 Discussion or later Results\n\n#### Guidelines\n\n- Plan 3-6 figures total across the paper\n- Place [FIGURE:fig_id] markers INLINE where referenced in text\n- Include axes, labels, ALL numeric values in figure descriptions\n- Both data-driven figures (bar charts, line plots) and conceptual diagrams (architecture, flowcharts)\n- Be as detailed as possible in descriptions: specify aspect ratio, preferred colors, all data values, axis labels, ranges, legend entries, and any other visual details. The more specific the description, the better the generated figure\n\n### Bibliography with Semantic Scholar\n\nBuild `./references.bib` using the aii-semscholar-bib skill (real BibTeX from Semantic Scholar):\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in one batch\n3. Write the returned .bib text into `./references.bib`\n\nRules:\n- Do NOT fabricate BibTeX entries \u2014 always fetch from Semantic Scholar\n- If a paper isn't found (very recent preprint), write the entry manually as fallback\n- Use `\\bibliography{references}` and `\\bibliographystyle{plainnat}`\n- Do NOT use inline `thebibliography` environment\n\n### Citation Format (for Research Artifacts)\n\nWhen writing research with numbered citations:\n\n1. Every factual claim MUST have a numbered citation: `[1]`, `[2]`, `[1, 3]`, etc.\n2. Each source in the \"sources\" array MUST have an \"index\" field\n3. The index MUST EXACTLY MATCH citation numbers in the text\n4. NEVER cite a number without a matching source index\n5. Example: \"LLMs show 40% improvement with multi-agent collaboration [1].\"\n\n---\nThis skill is located at `/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-paper-writing`. Any files it references (e.g. under `scripts/`, `references/`, `assets/`) are relative to that directory."}], "is_error": false, "skill_name": "aii-paper-writing", "kind": "InvokeSkillObservation"}
````

````
{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Tool: `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch`\n\nBatch-fetch BibTeX entries from Semantic Scholar. Pass all references in a single call \u2014 the tool handles batching internally.\n\n### How it works\n\n1. **DOI/ArXiv refs** \u2192 batched into POST /paper/batch calls (up to 500 per API call, auto-chunked)\n2. **Title-only refs** \u2192 individual GET /paper/search/match (1s delay between)\n3. **Post-process** \u2192 fix entry type, fix citation key (AuthorYYYY), inject DOI\n\nThe ability server runs a single worker (`max_threads: 1`). Multiple concurrent tool calls are queued \u2014 each runs independently (no cross-request aggregation). Batching happens within each request.\n\n### Input format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"references\": [\n    {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n    {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n    {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nEach reference object can have:\n- `doi` \u2014 DOI string (ArXiv DOIs like `10.48550/arXiv.XXXX.XXXXX` auto-convert to ArXiv IDs)\n- `arxiv` \u2014 ArXiv ID (e.g. `\"2305.14325\"`)\n- `title` \u2014 Paper title (used for search/match when no DOI/ArXiv)\n- `author` \u2014 First author last name (for cleaner citation key)\n- `year` \u2014 Publication year (int, for citation key)\n\nAt least one of `doi`, `arxiv`, or `title` is required per reference.\n\n### Output format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"success\": true,\n  \"bib_text\": \"@inproceedings{Vaswani2017, ...}\\n\\n@article{Wei2022, ...}\",\n  \"total\": 3,\n  \"found\": 3,\n  \"failed_count\": 0,\n  \"entries\": [{\"citation_key\": \"Vaswani2017\", \"bibtex\": \"...\", \"title\": \"...\", \"doi\": \"...\", \"arxiv\": \"\"}],\n  \"failed\": []\n}\n```\n\n### Workflow\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in **one call**\n3. Save `bib_text` from the response to your `references.bib` file\n4. Check `failed` \u2014 for any missed papers, follow the **fallback procedure** below\n\n### Fallback for failed references (MANDATORY)\n\nNEVER fabricate BibTeX. For each failed reference:\n1. **WebSearch** for `\"Title\" author year` (try `site:arxiv.org` too)\n2. **WebFetch** the paper page \u2192 extract title, authors, year, venue, DOI/ArXiv ID\n3. If DOI/ArXiv found \u2192 retry `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with it\n4. Textstat Library (Syllable Counting)**\\nTextstat is a popular Python library (PyPI package) that provides easy-to-use readability metrics [1]. For syllable counting, textstat uses Pyphen by default for most languages, but switches to NLTK's CMU pronouncing dictionary for en-US [1]. However, a critical GitHub issue (#195) reveals that textstat's syllable counting can be inaccurate - for example, it counts 'faeries' as 1 syllable (should be 2) and 'relived' as 3 syllables (should be 2) [2]. The issue notes that 'Pyphen is only 54% accurate,' suggesting significant limitations for research-grade applications [2].\\n\\n**2. CMU Pronouncing Dictionary (via pronouncing library)**\\nThe CMU Pronouncing Dictionary is an open-source machine-readable pronunciation dictionary containing over 134,000 words [3]. The `pronouncing` Python library provides a simple interface to query this dictionary [3]. Syllable counting with CMUdict is done by: (a) retrieving phoneme representations with `pronouncing.phones_for_word()`, and (b) counting vowels with stress markers using `pronouncing.syllable_count()` [3]. This method is MORE ACCURATE than heuristic approaches since it uses actual pronunciation data, but it may not cover all words (especially rare or domain-specific terms) [3].\\n\\n**3. Syllables Package (Heuristic-Based)**\\nThe `syllables` package (PyPI) provides fast syllable Estimation using heuristics [4]. It is designed for speed rather than accuracy - the documentation explicitly states: 'For situations where accuracy matters, please consider the cmudict Python library instead' [4]. This is a key trade-off: syllables is faster but less accurate than CMUdict-based approaches.\\n\\n**Recommendation for Research:** Use the CMU Pronouncing Dictionary via the `pronouncing` library or directly via NLTK's `cmudict` corpus reader for research-grade syllable counting. Have a fallback heuristic (like the `syllables` package) for out-of-vocabulary words.\\n\\n### Phase 2: Word Frequency Norm Sources\\n\\n**1. SUBTLEX-US (Recommended Primary Source)**\\nSUBTLEX-US provides word frequency norms based on 51 million words of American movie/TV subtitles [5]. It significantly outperforms older norms (Kucera & Francis 1967, Celex 1993) in predicting word processing times [5]. Key measures include:\\n- SUBTL WF: Word frequency per million words\\n- SUBTL CD: Percentage of films in which a word appears (contextual diversity) [5]\\n- Zipf scale values (1-7 range, easier interpretation) [5]\\n\\nThe SUBTLEX-US norms are available for free download (Excel/Text formats) from Ghent University [5]. An online lookup interface is available at subtlexus.lexique.org [5]. Part-of-Speech tagged versions are also available [5].\\n\\n**2. Google Books Ngrams**\\nThe Google Books Ngram corpus provides word frequency data from books digitized by Google [6]. While comprehensive, it has biases (published books, academic texts) and may not represent contemporary everyday language as well as SUBTLEX-US (which is based on subtitles) [5].\\n\\n**4. NLTK Corpora**\\nNLTK provides access to various corpora (Brown, Reuters, etc.) that can be used for frequency norms [7]. However, these are generally smaller and less representative than SUBTLEX-US.\\n\\n**Recommendation for Research:** Use SUBTLEX-US as the primary word frequency source. It is free, well-validated, and specifically designed for cognitive processing research. For words not in SUBTLEX-US, consider backing off to Google Books Ngrams or NLTK corpora.\\n\\n### Phase 3: Sentence-Level Readability Datasets\\n\\n**1. CLEAR Corpus (CommonLit Ease of Readability)**\\nThe CLEAR corpus is an open dataset of ~5,000 reading passage excerpts with readability scores [8]. Key properties:\\n- Grade levels: 3-12\\n- Scoring: Multiple readability indices (Flesch Reading Ease, Flesch-Kincaid, ARI, SMOG, Dale-Chall, CAREC, etc.) plus teacher ratings (Bradley-Terry coefficients) [8]\\n- Metadata: Author, title, publication year, genre (informational/literary), licensing info [8]\\n- Access: Google Sheets (MIT license for metadata) [8]\\n- Use: Successfully used in Kaggle Readability Prize competition\\n\\n**2. WSJ (Wall Street Journal) Dataset**\\nThe WSJ dataset contains 1,200 sentences graded on a difficulty scale of 1-7 [9]. Each sentence was rated by 20 native speakers [9]. The evaluated subset contains 650 sentences with \\u226514/20 annotator agreement [9]. This dataset is commonly used for sentence-level readability assessment research [9].\\n\\n**3. OneStopEnglish (OSE) Corpus**\\nOneStopEnglish contains 189 texts (567 total versions) at three reading grades: beginner, intermediate, advanced [10]. Each text has three aligned versions [10]. For sentence-level analysis, individual sentences are labeled based on human revision patterns (sentences that remain unchanged across levels retain the lower grade) [9].\\n\\n**4. Weekly Reader Corpus**\\nUsed in Feng et al. (2010), this corpus contains 1,433 articles for grades 2-5 from Weekly Reader magazine [11]. Articles are shorter than other corpora (average 128-336 words/document depending on grade) [11]. This corpus is particularly valuable for elementary school readability research.\\n\\n**Recommendation for Research:** For testing the 'Uniformity Principle' hypothesis, CLEAR corpus is recommended as the primary dataset because: (a) it provides sentence-level excerpts (not full documents), (b) it has multiple readability metrics, (c) it includes teacher ratings, and (d) it is open-access. WSJ dataset is also valuable for sentence-level analysis with human ratings.\\n\\n### Phase 4: Feature Landscape for Readability\\n\\n**Feng et al. Textstat Library (Syllable Counting)**\n    32\tTextstat is a popular Python library (PyPI package) that provides easy-to-use readability metrics [1]. For syllable counting, textstat uses Pyphen by default for most languages, but switches to NLTK's CMU pronouncing dictionary for en-US [1]. However, a critical GitHub issue (#195) reveals that textstat's syllable counting can be inaccurate - for example, it counts 'faeries' as 1 syllable (should be 2) and 'relived' as 3 syllables (should be 2) [2]. The issue notes that 'Pyphen is only 54% accurate,' suggesting significant limitations for research-grade applications [2].\n    33\t\n    34\t**2. CMU Pronouncing Dictionary (via pronouncing library)**\n    35\tThe CMU Pronouncing Dictionary is an open-source machine-readable pronunciation dictionary containing over 134,000 words [3]. The `pronouncing` Python library provides a simple interface to query this dictionary [3]. Syllable counting with CMUdict is done by: (a) retrieving phoneme representations with `pronouncing.phones_for_word()`, and (b) counting vowels with stress markers using `pronouncing.syllable_count()` [3]. This method is MORE ACCURATE than heuristic approaches since it uses actual pronunciation data, but it may not cover all words (especially rare or domain-specific terms) [3].\n    36\t\n    37\t**3. Syllables Package (Heuristic-Based)**\n    38\tThe `syllables` package (PyPI) provides fast syllable estimation using heuristics [4]. It is designed for speed rather than accuracy - the documentation explicitly states: 'For situations where accuracy matters, please consider the cmudict Python library instead' [4]. This is a key trade-off: syllables is faster but less accurate than CMUdict-based approaches.\n    39\t\n    40\t**Recommendation for Research:** Use the CMU Pronouncing Dictionary via the `pronouncing` library or directly via NLTK's `cmudict` corpus reader for research-grade syllable counting. Have a fallback heuristic (like the `syllables` package) for out-of-vocabulary words.\n    41\t\n    42\t### Phase 2: Word Frequency Norm Sources\n    43\t\n    44\t**1. It significantly outperforms older norms (Kucera & Francis 1967, Celex 1993) in predicting word processing times [5]. Key measures include:\n    46\t- SUBTL WF: Word frequency per million words\n    47\t- SUBTL CD: Percentage of films in which a word appears (contextual diversity) [5]\n    48\t- Zipf scale values (1-7 range, easier interpretation) [5]\n    49\t\n    50\tThe SUBTLEX-US norms are available for free download (Excel/Text formats) from Ghent University [5]. Let me create and run an experiment script that:
````
```
1. Computes word-level features (length, syllables, frequency)
2. Computes uniformity metrics (CV of these features)
3. Trains regression models to predict readability
4. Tests if uniformity features improve prediction
```

### [44] TOOL CALL — file_editor · 2026-07-21 14:52:02 UTC

```
{
  "command": "create",
  "path": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_nOuUUSNqdMp4/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_paper_text/gen_paper_text/run_experiment.py",
  "file_text": "#!/usr/bin/env python3\n\"\"\"\nExperiment script to test the Uniformity Principle hypothesis.\nComputes word-level features and uniformity metrics, then evaluates\nwhether uniformity features improve readability prediction.\n\nFeatures computed:\n- Word length in characters\n- Syllable count (via CMUdict + heuristic fallback)\n- Word frequency (via SUBTLEX-US or NLTK)\n- Uniformity: coefficient of variation (CV = std/mean) of each feature within sentence\n\"\"\"\n\nimport json\nimport sys\nimport os\nimport re\nfrom pathlib import Path\nfrom collections import defaultdict, Counter\nimport numpy as np\nfrom scipy import stats\nfrom sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score, KFold\nfrom sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression, Ridge\nfrom sklearn.metrics import mean_squared_error, r2_score\nfrom sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler\nimport warnings\nwarnings.filterwarnings('ignore')\n\n# Try importing required libraries\ntry:\n    from nltk.corpus import cmudict\n    import nltk\n    NLTK_AVAILABLE = True\nexcept ImportError:\n    NLTK_AVAILABLE = False\n\ntry:\n    import syllables\n    SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE = True\nexcept ImportError:\n    SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE = False\n\nprint(\"Libraries available: NLTK=\", NLTK_AVAILABLE, \"syllables=\", SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE)\n\n# Download NLTK data if needed\nif NLTK_AVAILABLE:\n    try:\n        nltk.data.find('corpora/cmudict.zip')\n    except LookupError:\n        print(\"Downloading CMUdict...\")\n        nltk.download('cmudict', quiet=True)\n\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n# Feature Computation Functions\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n\ndef get_syllable_count_cmudict(word, cmu_dict):\n    \"\"\"Get syllable count using CMU Pronouncing Dictionary.\"\"\"\n    word_lower = word.lower()\n    if word_lower in cmu_dict:\n        pronunciations = cmu_dict[word_lower]\n        # Use first pronunciation, count vowel phonemes (digits indicate stress)\n        phones = pronunciations[0]\n        syllable_count = len([p for p in phones if any(c.isdigit() for c in p)])\n        return syllable_count\n    return None  # OOV\n\ndef get_syllable_count_heuristic(word):\n    \"\"\"Heuristic syllable counting fallback.\"\"\"\n    word = word.lower()\n    if len(word) <= 3:\n        return 1\n    # Remove trailing 'e'\n    word = re.sub(r'e$', '', word)\n    # Count vowel groups\n    vowels = 'aeiouy'\n    count = 0\n    prev_was_vowel = False\n    for char in word:\n        is_vowel = char in vowels\n        if is_vowel and not prev_was_vowel:\n            count += 1\n        prev_was_vowel = is_vowel\n    return max(1, count)\n\ndef get_syllable_count(word, cmu_dict):\n    \"\"\"Get syllable count with CMUdict primary, heuristic fallback.\"\"\"\n    if NLTK_AVAILABLE and cmu_dict:\n        sc = get_syllable_count_cmudict(word, cmu_dict)\n        if sc is not None:\n            return sc\n    if SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE:\n        try:\n            return syllables.estimate(word)\n        except:\n            pass\n    return get_syllable_count_heuristic(word)\n\ndef get_word_frequency(word, freq_dict):\n    \"\"\"Get word frequency from dictionary. Returns log frequency or default.\"\"\"\n    word_lower = word.lower()\n    if word_lower in freq_dict:\n        return freq_dict[word_lower]\n    # Return a default low frequency for OOV words\n    return 1  # frequency count of 1 (rarest)\n\ndef build_freq_dict():\n    \"\"\"Build a simple word frequency dictionary from NLTK's Gutenberg corpus.\"\"\"\n    freq_dict = defaultdict(int)\n    \n    # Try NLTK corpora\n    if NLTK_AVAILABLE:\n        try:\n            from nltk.corpus import gutenberg\n            for fileid in gutenberg.fileids():\n                for word in gutenberg.words(fileid):\n                    freq_dict[word.lower()] += 1\n            print(f\"Built frequency dict from Gutenberg: {len(freq_dict)} words\")\n            # Convert to log frequency\n            log_freq_dict = {w: np.log1p(f) for w, f in freq_dict.items()}\n            return log_freq_dict\n        except Exception as e:\n            print(f\"Gutenberg corpus not available: {e}\")\n    \n    # Fallback: use a simple zipf-based frequency\n    print(\"Using heuristic frequency estimation\")\n    return freq_dict\n\ndef tokenize_words(sentence):\n    \"\"\"Simple word tokenization.\"\"\"\n    # Remove punctuation and split\n    words = re.findall(r'\\b[a-zA-Z]+\\b', sentence)\n    return words\n\ndef compute_sentence_features(sentence, cmu_dict, freq_dict):\n    \"\"\"\n    Compute all features for a sentence.\n    Returns dict with:\n    - avg_word_len, cv_word_len\n    - avg_syllables, cv_syllables  \n    - avg_freq, cv_freq\n    - num_words\n    \"\"\"\n    words = tokenize_words(sentence)\n    if len(words) < 2:\n        return None  # Need at least 2 words to compute CV\n    \n    # Word lengths\n    word_lengths = [len(w) for w in words]\n    \n    # Syllable counts\n    syllable_counts = [get_syllable_count(w, cmu_dict) for w in words]\n    \n    # Word frequencies (log)\n    word_freqs = [get_word_frequency(w, freq_dict) for w in words]\n    \n    features = {}\n    \n    # Averages (traditional readability features)\n    features['avg_word_len'] = np.mean(word_lengths)\n    features['avg_syllables'] = np.mean(syllable_counts)\n    features['avg_freq'] = np.mean(word_freqs)  # Higher = more frequent = easier\n    features['num_words'] = len(words)\n    \n    # Coefficient of variation (uniformity features)\n    # CV = std / mean (normalized measure of dispersion)\n    if features['avg_word_len'] > 0:\n        features['cv_word_len'] = np.std(word_lengths) / features['avg_word_len']\n    else:\n        features['cv_word_len'] = 0\n        \n    if features['avg_syllables'] > 0:\n        features['cv_syllables'] = np.std(syllable_counts) / features['avg_syllables']\n    else:\n        features['cv_syllables'] = 0\n    \n    if features['avg_freq'] > 0:\n        features['cv_freq'] = np.std(word_freqs) / features['avg_freq']\n    else:\n        features['cv_freq'] = 0\n    \n    # Also compute normalized variance (just std for comparison)\n    features['std_word_len'] = np.std(word_lengths)\n    features['std_syllables'] = np.std(syllable_counts)\n    features['std_freq'] = np.std(word_freqs)\n    \n    return features\n\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n# Experiment Runner\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n\ndef run_experiment(data_file, output_file):\n    \"\"\"Run the full experiment on a dataset.\"\"\"\n    \n    print(f\"\\n=== Loading data from {data_file} ===\")\n    with open(data_file) as f:\n        data = json.load(f)\n    \n    # Initialize CMU dict and frequency dict\n    cmu_dict = None\n    if NLTK_AVAILABLE:\n        try:\n            cmu_dict = cmudict.dict()\n            print(\"Loaded CMU Pronouncing Dictionary\")\n        except Exception as e:\n            print(f\"Could not load CMUdict: {e}\")\n    \n    freq_dict = build_freq_dict()\n    \n    results = {}\n    \n    for dataset in data['datasets']:\n        dataset_name = dataset['dataset']\n        examples = dataset['examples']\n        \n        print(f\"\\n=== Processing {dataset_name} ({len(examples)} examples) ===\")\n        \n        # Compute features for each sentence\n        all_features = []\n        all_scores = []\n        skipped = 0\n        \n        for i, ex in enumerate(examples):\n            sentence = ex['input']\n            # Readability score: lower = easier, higher = harder\n            # In our data: \"0.0\" = easy, higher values = harder\n            try:\n                score = float(ex['output'])\n            except:\n                # Skip if no valid score\n                skipped += 1\n                continue\n            \n            features = compute_sentence_features(sentence, cmu_dict, freq_dict)\n            if features is None:\n                skipped += 1\n                continue\n                \n            all_features.append(features)\n            all_scores.append(score)\n        \n        print(f\"  Computed features for {len(all_features)} sentences (skipped {skipped})\")\n        \n        if len(all_features) < 100:\n            print(f\"  WARNING: Only {len(all_features)} valid examples, skipping dataset\")\n            continue\n        \n        # Convert to arrays\n        feature_names = list(all_features[0].keys())\n        X = np.array([[f[n] for n in feature_names] for f in all_features])\n        y = np.array(all_scores)\n        \n        print(f\"  Feature matrix shape: {X.shape}\")\n        print(f\"  Score range: [{y.min():.3f}, {y.max():.3f}]\")\n        \n        # Store for this dataset\n        results[dataset_name] = {\n            'feature_names': feature_names,\n            'X': X,\n            'y': y,\n            'n_samples': len(y)\n        }\n    \n    # Run model comparisons\n    print(\"\\n=== Model Comparison Results ===\")\n    \n    experiment_results = []\n    \n    for dataset_name, data in results.items():\n        X = data['X']\n        y = data['y']\n        feature_names = data['feature_names']\n        \n        print(f\"\\n--- {dataset_name} ({data['n_samples']} samples) ---\")\n        \n        # Identify feature groups\n        avg_features = [i for i, n in enumerate(feature_names) if n.startswith('avg_') or n == 'num_words']\n        cv_features = [i for i, n in enumerate(feature_names) if n.startswith('cv_')]\n        std_features = [i for i, n in enumerate(feature_names) if n.startswith('std_')]\n        \n        print(f\"  Average features ({len(avg_features)}): {[feature_names[i] for i in avg_features]}\")\n        print(f\"  CV features ({len(cv_features)}): {[feature_names[i] for i in cv_features]}\")\n        \n        # Standardize features\n        scaler = StandardScaler()\n        \n        # Model 1: Only average features (traditional)\n        X_avg = X[:, avg_features]\n        X_avg_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X_avg)\n        \n        # Model 2: Only uniformity features (CV)\n        X_cv = X[:, cv_features]\n        X_cv_scaled = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X_cv)\n        \n        # Model 3: Combined (average + CV)\n        X_comb = np.hstack([X_avg, X_cv])\n        X_comb_scaled = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X_comb)\n        \n        # Model 4: All features\n        X_all_scaled = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X)\n        \n        # Cross-validated R\u00b2 comparison\n        cv = KFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42)\n        \n        models = {\n            'Average only': (X_avg_scaled, \"Traditional readability features\"),\n            'Uniformity only (CV)': (X_cv_scaled, \"CV of word-level features\"),\n            'Average + Uniformity': (X_comb_scaled, \"Combined features\"),\n        }\n        \n        dataset_results = {\n            'dataset': dataset_name,\n            'n_samples': data['n_samples'],\n            'models': {}\n        }\n        \n        for model_name, (X_model, description) in models.items():\n            # Use Ridge regression for stability\n            model = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n            scores = cross_val_score(model, X_model, y, cv=cv, scoring='r2')\n            mse_scores = cross_val_score(model, X_model, y, cv=cv, scoring='neg_mean_squared_error')\n            mae_scores = cross_val_score(model, X_model, y, cv=cv, scoring='neg_mean_absolute_error')\n            \n            r2_mean = scores.mean()\n            r2_std = scores.std()\n            mse_mean = -mse_scores.mean()\n            mae_mean = -mae_scores.mean()\n            \n            dataset_results['models'][model_name] = {\n                'r2_mean': r2_mean,\n                'r2_std': r2_std,\n                'mse_mean': mse_mean,\n                'mae_mean': mae_mean\n            }\n            \n            print(f\"  {model_name:30s}: R\u00b2 = {r2_mean:.4f} \u00b1 {r2_std:.4f}, MSE = {mse_mean:.4f}, MAE = {mae_mean:.4f}\")\n        \n        # Statistical significance test: compare \"Average only\" vs \"Average + Uniformity\"\n        # Use paired bootstrap test\n        X_base = X_avg_scaled\n        X_full = X_comb_scaled\n        \n        # Fit both models on full data for comparison\n        model_base = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n        model_full = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n        \n        # Bootstrap test for significance\n        n_bootstrap = 1000\n        r2_diff_bootstrap = []\n        \n        for b in range(n_bootstrap):\n            indices = np.random.choice(len(y), size=len(y), replace=True)\n            Xb_base = X_base[indices]\n            Xb_full = X_full[indices]\n            yb = y[indices]\n            \n            model_base.fit(Xb_base, yb)\n            model_full.fit(Xb_full, yb)\n            \n            r2_base = model_base.score(Xb_base, yb)\n            r2_full = model_full.score(Xb_full, yb)\n            r2_diff_bootstrap.append(r2_full - r2_base)\n        \n        r2_diff_bootstrap = np.array(r2_diff_bootstrap)\n        p_value = (np.sum(r2_diff_bootstrap <= 0) + 1) / (n_bootstrap + 1)  # One-sided test\n        \n        r2_base = dataset_results['models']['Average only']['r2_mean']\n        r2_full = dataset_results['models']['Average + Uniformity']['r2_mean']\n        r2_improvement = r2_full - r2_base\n        \n        print(f\"  Bootstrap significance test (Average vs Combined):\")\n        print(f\"    R\u00b2 improvement: {r2_improvement:.4f}\")\n        print(f\"    p-value (one-sided): {p_value:.4f}\")\n        \n        dataset_results['bootstrap'] = {\n            'r2_improvement': r2_improvement,\n            'p_value': p_value\n        }\n        \n        # Feature importance analysis\n        model_full_all = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n        model_full_all.fit(X_comb_scaled, y)\n        \n        feature_importance = model_full_all.coef_\n        combined_feature_names = [feature_names[i] for i in avg_features] + [feature_names[i] for i in cv_features]\n        \n        print(f\"  Feature importance (top 10):\")\n        importance_sorted = sorted(zip(combined_feature_names, feature_importance), key=lambda x: abs(x[1]), reverse=True)\n        for fname, imp in importance_sorted[:10]:\n            print(f\"    {fname:30s}: {imp:+.4f}\")\n        \n        dataset_results['feature_importance'] = [\n            {'feature': fname, 'coefficient': imp} \n            for fname, imp in zip(combined_feature_names, feature_importance)\n        ]\n        \n        experiment_results.append(dataset_results)\n    \n    # Save results\n    print(f\"\\n=== Saving results to {output_file} ===\")\n    \n    # Convert numpy types to Python types for JSON serialization\n    def convert_for_json(obj):\n        if isinstance(obj, np.ndarray):\n            return obj.tolist()\n        elif isinstance(obj, np.integer):\n            return int(obj)\n        elif isinstance(obj, np.floating):\n            return float(obj)\n        elif isinstance(obj, dict):\n            return {k: convert_for_json(v) for k, v in obj.items()}\n        elif isinstance(obj, list):\n            return [convert_for_json(item) for item in obj]\n        else:\n            return obj\n    \n    output_data = {\n        'experiment_results': convert_for_json(experiment_results),\n        'summary': {\n            'datasets_processed': list(results.keys()),\n            'total_samples': sum(d['n_samples'] for d in results.values())\n        }\n    }\n    \n    with open(output_file, 'w') as f:\n        json.dump(output_data, f, indent=2)\n    \n    print(\"Done!\")\n    return output_data\n\nif __name__ == '__main__':\n    data_file = '/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_nOuUUSNqdMp4/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_dataset_1/full_data_out.json'\n    output_file = 'experiment_results.json'\n    \n    # prediction.\n\nFeatures computed:\n- Word length in characters\n- Syllable count (via CMUdict + heuristic fallback)\n- Word frequency (via SUBTLEX-US or NLTK)\n- Uniformity: coefficient of variation (CV = std/mean) of each feature within sentence\n\"\"\"\n\nimport json\nimport sys\nimport os\nimport re\nfrom pathlib import Path\nfrom collections import defaultdict, Counter\nimport numpy as import nltk\n    NLTK_AVAILABLE = True\nexcept ImportError:\n    NLTK_AVAILABLE = False\n\ntry:\n    import syllables\n    SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE = True\nexcept ImportError:\n    SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE = False\n\nprint(\"Libraries available: NLTK=\", NLTK_AVAILABLE, \"syllables=\", SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE)\n\n# Download NLTK data if needed\nif NLTK_AVAILABLE:\n    try:\n ------------------------------------------------------------\n# Feature Computation Functions\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n\ndef get_syllable_count_cmudict(word, cmu_dict):\n    \"\"\"Get syllable count using CMU Pronouncing Dictionary.\"\"\"\n    word_lower = word.lower()\n    if word_lower in cmu_dict:\n        pronunciations = cmu_dict[word_lower]\n        # Use first pronunciation, count vowel phonemes (digits indicate stress)\n        phones = pronunciations[0]\n        syllable_count = len([p for p in phones if any(c.isdigit() for c in p)])\n        return syllable_count\n    return None  # OOV\n\ndef get_syllable_count_heuristic(word):\n    \"\"\"Heuristic syllable counting fallback.\"\"\"\n    word = word.lower()\n    if len(word) <= 3:\n        return 1\n    # Remove trailing 'e'\n    word = re.sub(r'e$', '', word)\n    # Count vowel groups\n    vowels = 'aeiouy'\n    count = 0\n    prev_was_vowel = False\n    for char in word:\n        is_vowel = char in vowels\n        if is_vowel and not prev_was_vowel:\n            count += 1\n        prev_was_vowel = is_vowel\n    return max(1, count)\n\ndef get_syllable_count(word, cmu_dict):\n    \"\"\"Get syllable count with CMUdict primary, heuristic fallback.\"\"\"\n    if NLTK_AVAILABLE and cmu_dict:\n        sc = get_syllable_count_cmudict(word, cmu_dict)\n        if sc is not None:\n            return sc\n    if SYLLABLES_AVAILABLE:\n        try:\n            return syllables.estimate(word)\n        except:\n            pass\n    return get_syllable_count_heuristic(word)\n\ndef get_word_frequency(word, freq_dict):\n    \"\"\"Get word frequency from dictionary. Returns log frequency or default.\"\"\"\n    word_lower = word.lower()\n    if word_lower in freq_dict:\n        return freq_dict[word_lower]\n    # Return a default low frequency for OOV words\n    return 1  # frequency count of 1 (rarest)\n\ndef build_freq_dict():\n    \"\"\"Build a simple word frequency dictionary from NLTK's Gutenberg corpus.\"\"\"\n    freq_dict = defaultdict(int)\n    \n    # Try NLTK corpora\n    if NLTK_AVAILABLE:\n        try:\n            from nltk.corpus import gutenberg\n            for fileid in gutenberg.fileids():\n                for word in gutenberg.words(fileid):\n                    freq_dict[word.lower()] += 1\n            print(f\"Built frequency dict from Gutenberg: {len(freq_dict)} words\")\n            # Convert to log frequency\n            log_freq_dict = {w: np.log1p(f) for w, f in freq_dict.items()}\n            return log_freq_dict\n        except Exception as e:\n            print(f\"Gutenberg corpus not available: {e}\")\n    \n    # Fallback: use a simple zipf-based frequency\n    print(\"Using heuristic frequency estimation\")\n    return freq_dict\n\ndef tokenize_words(sentence):\n    \"\"\"Simple word tokenization.\"\"\"\n    # Remove punctuation and split\n    words = re.findall(r'\\b[a-zA-Z]+\\b', sentence)\n    return words\n\ndef compute_sentence_features(sentence, cmu_dict, freq_dict):\n    \"\"\"\n    Compute all features for a sentence.\n    Returns dict with:\n    - avg_word_len, cv_word_len\n    - avg_syllables, cv_syllables  \n    - avg_freq, cv_freq\n    - num_words\n    \"\"\"\n    words = tokenize_words(sentence)\n    if len(words) < 2:\n        return None  # Need at least 2 words to compute CV\n    \n    # Word lengths\n    word_lengths = [len(w) for w in words]\n    \n    # Syllable counts\n    syllable_counts = [get_syllable_count(w, cmu_dict) for w in words]\n    \n    # Word frequencies (log)\n    word_freqs = [get_word_frequency(w, freq_dict) for w in words]\n    \n    features = {}\n    \n    # Averages (traditional readability features)\n    features['avg_word_len'] = np.mean(word_lengths)\n    features['avg_syllables'] = np.mean(syllable_counts)\n    features['avg_freq'] = np.mean(word_freqs)  # Higher = more frequent = easier\n    features['num_words'] = len(words)\n    \n    # Coefficient of variation (uniformity features)\n    # CV = std / mean (normalized measure of dispersion)\n    if features['avg_word_len'] > 0:\n        features['cv_word_len'] = np.std(word_lengths) / features['avg_word_len']\n    else:\n        features['cv_word_len'] = 0\n        \n    if features['avg_syllables'] > 0:\n        features['cv_syllables'] = np.std(syllable_counts) / features['avg_syllables']\n    else:\n        features['cv_syllables'] = 0\n    \n    if features['avg_freq'] > 0:\n        features['cv_freq'] = np.std(word_freqs) / features['avg_freq']\n    else:\n        features['cv_freq'] = 0\n    \n    # Also compute normalized variance (just std for comparison)\n    features['std_word_len'] = np.std(word_lengths)\n    features['std_syllables'] = np.std(syllable_counts)\n    features['std_freq'] = np.std(word_freqs)\n    \n    return features\n\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n# Experiment Runner\n# ------------------------------------------------------------\n\ndef run_experiment(data_file, output_file):\n    \"\"\"Run the full experiment on a dataset.\"\"\"\n    \n    print(f\"\\n=== Loading data from {data_file} ===\")\n    with open(data_file) as f:\n        data = json.load(f)\n    \n    # Initialize CMU dict and frequency dict\n    cmu_dict = None\n    if NLTK_AVAILABLE:\n        try:\n            cmu_dict = cmudict.dict()\n            print(\"Loaded CMU Pronouncing Dictionary\")\n        except Exception as e:\n            print(f\"Could not load CMUdict: {e}\")\n    \n    freq_dict = build_freq_dict()\n    \n    results = {}\n    \n    for dataset in data['datasets']:\n        dataset_name = dataset['dataset']\n        examples = dataset['examples']\n        \n        print(f\"\\n=== Processing {dataset_name} ({len(examples)} examples) ===\")\n        \n        # Compute features for each sentence\n        all_features = []\n        all_scores = []\n        skipped = 0\n        \n        for i, ex in enumerate(examples):\n            sentence = ex['input']\n            # Readability score: lower = easier, higher = harder\n            # In our data: \"0.0\" = easy, higher values = harder\n            try:\n                score = float(ex['output'])\n            except:\n                # Skip if no valid score\n                skipped += 1\n                continue\n            \n            features = compute_sentence_features(sentence, cmu_dict, freq_dict)\n            if features is None:\n                skipped += 1\n                continue\n                \n            all_features.append(features)\n            all_scores.append(score)\n        \n        print(f\"  Computed features for {len(all_features)} sentences (skipped {skipped})\")\n        \n        if len(all_features) < 100:\n            print(f\"  WARNING: Only {len(all_features)} valid examples, skipping dataset\")\n            continue\n        \n        # Convert to arrays\n        feature_names = list(all_features[0].keys())\n        X = np.array([[f[n] for n in feature_names] for f in all_features])\n        y = np.array(all_scores)\n        \n        print(f\"  Feature matrix shape: {X.shape}\")\n        print(f\"  Score range: [{y.min():.3f}, {y.max():.3f}]\")\n        \n        # Store for this dataset\n        results[dataset_name] = {\n            'feature_names': feature_names,\n            'X': X,\n            'y': y,\n            'n_samples': len(y)\n        }\n    \n    # Run model comparisons\n    print(\"\\n=== Model Comparison Results ===\")\n    \n    experiment_results = []\n    \n    for dataset_name, data in results.items():\n        X = data['X']\n        y = data['y']\n        feature_names = data['feature_names']\n        \n        print(f\"\\n--- {dataset_name} ({data['n_samples']} samples) ---\")\n        \n        # Identify feature groups\n        avg_features = [i for i, n in enumerate(feature_names) if n.startswith('avg_') or n == 'num_words']\n        cv_features = [i for i, n in enumerate(feature_names) if n.startswith('cv_')]\n        std_features = [i for i, n in enumerate(feature_names) if n.startswith('std_')]\n        \n        print(f\"  Average features ({len(avg_features)}): {[feature_names[i] for i in avg_features]}\")\n        print(f\"  CV features ({len(cv_features)}): {[feature_names[i] for i in cv_features]}\")\n        \n        # Standardize features\n        scaler = StandardScaler()\n        \n        # Model 1: Only average features (traditional)\n        X_avg = X[:, avg_features]\n        X_avg_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X_avg)\n        \n        # Model 2: Only uniformity features (CV)\n        X_cv = X[:, cv_features]\n        X_cv_scaled = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X_cv)\n        \n        # Model 3: Combined (average + CV)\n        X_comb = np.hstack([X_avg, X_cv])\n        X_comb_scaled = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X_comb)\n        \n        # Model 4: All features\n        X_all_scaled = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X)\n        \n        # Cross-validated R\u00b2 comparison\n        cv = KFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42)\n        \n        models = {\n            'Average only': (X_avg_scaled, \"Traditional readability features\"),\n            'Uniformity only (CV)': (X_cv_scaled, \"CV of word-level features\"),\n            'Average + Uniformity': (X_comb_scaled, \"Combined features\"),\n        }\n        \n        dataset_results = {\n            'dataset': dataset_name,\n            'n_samples': data['n_samples'],\n            'models': {}\n        }\n        \n        for model_name, (X_model, description) in models.items():\n            # Use Ridge regression for stability\n            model = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n            scores = cross_val_score(model, X_model, y, cv=cv, scoring='r2')\n            mse_scores = cross_val_score(model, X_model, y, cv=cv, scoring='neg_mean_squared_error')\n            mae_scores = cross_val_score(model, X_model, y, cv=cv, scoring='neg_mean_absolute_error')\n            \n            r2_mean = scores.mean()\n            r2_std = scores.std()\n            mse_mean = -mse_scores.mean()\n            mae_mean = -mae_scores.mean()\n            \n            dataset_results['models'][model_name] = {\n                'r2_mean': r2_mean,\n                'r2_std': r2_std,\n                'mse_mean': mse_mean,\n                'mae_mean': mae_mean\n            }\n            \n            print(f\"  {model_name:30s}: R\u00b2 = {r2_mean:.4f} \u00b1 {r2_std:.4f}, MSE = {mse_mean:.4f}, MAE = {mae_mean:.4f}\")\n        \n        # Statistical significance test: compare \"Average only\" vs \"Average + Uniformity\"\n        # Use paired bootstrap test\n        X_base = X_avg_scaled\n        X_full = X_comb_scaled\n        \n        # Fit both models on full data for comparison\n        model_base = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n        model_full = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n        \n        # Bootstrap test for significance\n        n_bootstrap = 1000\n        r2_diff_bootstrap = []\n        \n        for b in range(n_bootstrap):\n            indices = np.random.choice(len(y), size=len(y), replace=True)\n            Xb_base = X_base[indices]\n            Xb_full = X_full[indices]\n            yb = y[indices]\n            \n            model_base.fit(Xb_base, yb)\n            model_full.fit(Xb_full, yb)\n            \n            r2_base = model_base.score(Xb_base, yb)\n            r2_full = model_full.score(Xb_full, yb)\n            r2_diff_bootstrap.append(r2_full - r2_base)\n        \n        r2_diff_bootstrap = np.array(r2_diff_bootstrap)\n        p_value = (np.sum(r2_diff_bootstrap <= 0) + 1) / (n_bootstrap + 1)  # One-sided test\n        \n        r2_base = dataset_results['models']['Average only']['r2_mean']\n        r2_full = dataset_results['models']['Average + Uniformity']['r2_mean']\n        r2_improvement = r2_full - r2_base\n        \n        print(f\"  Bootstrap significance test (Average vs Combined):\")\n        print(f\"    R\u00b2 improvement: {r2_improvement:.4f}\")\n        print(f\"    p-value (one-sided): {p_value:.4f}\")\n        \n        dataset_results['bootstrap'] = {\n            'r2_improvement': r2_improvement,\n            'p_value': p_value\n        }\n        \n        # Feature importance analysis\n        model_full_all = Ridge(alpha=1.0, random_state=42)\n        model_full_all.fit(X_comb_scaled, y)\n        \n        feature_importance = model_full_all.coef_\n        combined_feature_names = [feature_names[i] for i in avg_features] + [feature_names[i] for i in cv_features]\n        \n        print(f\"  Feature importance (top 10):\")\n        importance_sorted = sorted(zip(combined_feature_names, feature_importance), key=lambda x: abs(x[1]), reverse=True)\n        for fname, imp in importance_sorted[:10]:\n            print(f\"    {fname:30s}: {imp:+.4f}\")\n        \n        dataset_results['feature_importance'] = [\n            {'feature': fname, 'coefficient': imp} \n            for fname, imp in zip(combined_feature_names, feature_importance)\n        ]\n        \n        experiment_results.append(dataset_results)\n    \n    #
```
